   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Earth Day</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Annual event on April 22
      </div> 
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p> 
      <table class="infobox vevent">
       <tbody>

        </tr>
        <tr>

          <div class="infobox-caption" style="caption">

        </tr>

      </table> 
      <p><b>Earth Day</b> is an annual event on April 22 to demonstrate support for <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a>. First held on April 22, 1970, it now includes a wide range of events coordinated globally by EarthDay.org (formerly Earth Day Network)<sup id="cite_ref-:1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1">[1]</a></sup> including 1 billion people in more than 193 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1969 at a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> Conference in San Francisco, <a href="/wiki/Peace_activist" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace activist">peace activist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_McConnell_(peace_activist)" title="John McConnell (peace activist)">John McConnell</a> proposed a day to honor the Earth and the concept of peace, to first be observed on March 21, 1970, the <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)#Astronomical_and_solar_reckoning" title="Spring (season)">first day of spring</a> in the northern hemisphere. This day of nature's equipoise was later sanctioned in a proclamation written by McConnell and signed by Secretary General <a href="/wiki/U_Thant" title="U Thant">U Thant</a> at the United Nations. A month later United States Senator <a href="/wiki/Gaylord_Nelson" title="Gaylord Nelson">Gaylord Nelson</a> proposed the idea to hold a nationwide environmental <a href="/wiki/Teach-in" title="Teach-in">teach-in</a> on April 22, 1970. He hired a young activist, <a href="/wiki/Denis_Hayes" title="Denis Hayes">Denis Hayes</a>, to be the National Coordinator. Nelson and Hayes renamed the event "Earth Day". Denis and his staff grew the event beyond the original idea for a teach-in to include the entire United States. More than 20 million people poured out on the streets, and the first Earth Day remains the largest single day protest in human history. Key non-environmentally focused partners played major roles. Under the leadership of labor leader <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a>, for example, the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a> (UAW) was the most instrumental outside financial and operational supporter of the first Earth Day.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> According to Hayes, "Without the UAW, the first Earth Day would have likely flopped!"<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Nelson was later awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> award in recognition of his work.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first Earth Day was focused on the United States. In 1990, Denis Hayes, the original national coordinator in 1970, took it international and organized events in 141 nations.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On Earth Day 2016, the landmark <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> was signed by the United States, the United Kingdom, China, and 120 other countries. This signing satisfied a key requirement for the <a href="/wiki/Coming_into_force" title="Coming into force">entry into force</a> of the historic draft <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">climate protection treaty</a> adopted by consensus of the 195 nations present at the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference</a> in Paris. </p>
      <p>Numerous communities engaged in <b>Earth Day Week actions</b>, an entire week of activities focused on the environmental issues that the world faces.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-11">[11]</a></sup> On Earth Day 2020, over 100 million people around the world observed the 50th anniversary in what is being referred to as the largest online mass mobilization in history.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1969_Santa_Barbara_Oil_Spill">1969 Santa Barbara Oil Spill</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/1969_Santa_Barbara_oil_spill" title="1969 Santa Barbara oil spill">1969 Santa Barbara oil spill</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On January 28, 1969, a well drilled by Union Oil Platform A off the coast of Santa Barbara, California, blew out. More than three million gallons of oil spewed, killing more than 10,000 seabirds, dolphins, seals, and sea lions. As a reaction to this disaster, activists were mobilized to create environmental regulation, environmental education, and Earth Day. Among the proponents of Earth Day were the people in the front lines of fighting this disaster, <a href="/wiki/Selma_Rubin" title="Selma Rubin">Selma Rubin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_McGinnes" title="Marc McGinnes">Marc McGinnes</a>, and Bud Bottoms, founder of Get Oil Out.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Hayes" title="Denis Hayes">Denis Hayes</a>, organizer of the first Earth Day said that Senator Gaylord Nelson from Wisconsin was inspired to create Earth Day upon seeing Santa Barbara Channel 800 square-mile oil slick from an airplane.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:15_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-13">[13]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Santa_Barbara.27s_Environmental_Rights_Day_1970"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Santa_Barbara's_Environmental_Rights_Day_1970">Santa Barbara's Environmental Rights Day 1970</span></h2> 
      <p>On the first anniversary of the oil blowout, January 28, 1970, Environmental Rights Day was created and the Declaration of Environmental Rights was read. It had been written by Rod Nash during a boat trip across the Santa Barbara Channel while carrying a copy of Thomas Jefferson's Declaration of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-12">[12]</a></sup> The organizers of Environmental Rights Day, led by <a href="/wiki/Marc_McGinnes" title="Marc McGinnes">Marc McGinnes</a>, had been working closely over a period of several months with Congressman <a href="/wiki/Pete_McCloskey" title="Pete McCloskey">Pete McCloskey</a> (R-CA) to consult on the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act</a>, the first of many new environmental protection laws sparked by the national outcry about the blowout/oil spill and on the Declaration of Environmental Rights. Both McCloskey (Earth Day co-chair with Senator <a href="/wiki/Gaylord_Nelson" title="Gaylord Nelson">Gaylord Nelson</a>) and Earth Day organizer <a href="/wiki/Denis_Hayes" title="Denis Hayes">Denis Hayes</a>, along with Senator <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cranston" title="Alan Cranston">Alan Cranston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Brower" title="David Brower">David Brower</a> and other prominent leaders, endorsed the Declaration and spoke about it at the Environmental Rights Day conference. According to Francis Sarguis, "the conference was sort of like the baptism for the movement." According to Hayes, this was the first giant crowd he spoke to that "felt passionately, I mean really passionately, about environmental issues." Hayes also thought the conference might be the beginning of a real movement.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-12">[12]</a></sup> Nash, <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Hardin" title="Garrett Hardin">Garrett Hardin</a>, McGinnes and others went on to develop the first undergraduate Environmental Studies program of its kind at the University of California at Santa Barbara.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-14">[14]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_1970">Earth Day 1970</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg/220px-Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg/330px-Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg/440px-Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5704" data-file-height="3824"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nixons_plant_a_tree_C6311-11a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> and First Lady <a href="/wiki/Pat_Nixon" title="Pat Nixon">Pat Nixon</a> plant a tree on the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> South Lawn to recognize the first Earth Day.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The seeds that grew into the first Earth Day were planted by Wisconsin Senator Gaylord Nelson. An ardent conservationist and former two-term governor of Wisconsin, Nelson had long sought ways to increase the potency of the environment as a political issue. The extraordinary attention garnered by Rachel Carson’s book, <i><a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">Silent Spring</a></i>, the famous <a href="/wiki/Earthrise#/media/File:NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg" title="Earthrise">1968 Earthrise NASA photograph</a> of the earth from the moon, the saturation news coverage given to the Santa Barbara oil spill<sup id="cite_ref-:16aa_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16aa-15">[15]</a></sup> and the Cuyahoga River catching fire in early 1969<sup id="cite_ref-:16bb_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16bb-16">[16]</a></sup> led Nelson to think the time was ripe for an environmental initiative. As a result of interactions with his staff and with <a href="/wiki/Fred_Dutton" title="Fred Dutton">Fred Dutton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:16mm_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16mm-17">[17]</a></sup> a prominent Democratic operative who had been Robert Kennedy’s Presidential campaign manager, Nelson became convinced that environmental teach-ins on college campuses could serve as such a vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Teach-Ins had been held on hundreds of college campuses to debate the war in Vietnam. They generally reflected the divide between those who thought of Vietnam as a bulwark to stop additional countries falling to communism like <a href="/wiki/Domino_theory" title="Domino theory">dominos</a>, versus those who believed that the war was the latest stage of a nationalist, anti-colonialist campaign<sup id="cite_ref-:16bdd_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16bdd-19">[19]</a></sup> by Vietnamese who had fought against China, then France, Japan, France again, and now the United States. These debates elevated arguments over the war in the public consciousness and enlisted a generation of student activists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Nelson asked public interest lawyer Anthony Roisman<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> to establish a non-profit, Environmental Teach-In, Inc., to manage the campaign, and recruited a small board of directors. He asked Republican Congressman Pete McCloskey<sup id="cite_ref-:13_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-11">[11]</a></sup> to co-chair the board to ensure it was bipartisan and bicameral.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On September 20, 1969, Senator Nelson first announced his plans for an “environmental teach-In” in a little publicized talk at the University of Washington. “I am convinced that the same concern the youth of this nation took in changing this nation’s priorities on the war in Vietnam and on civil rights can be shown for the problem of the environment. That is why I plan to see to it that a national teach-in is held.”<sup id="cite_ref-:15_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Senator Nelson went on to encourage teach-ins at many more speeches. A November talk at Airlie House had a <i>New York Times</i> reporter in the audience. The resulting front page article<sup id="cite_ref-:16_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-14">[14]</a></sup> was a turning point. Letters of inquiry from across the country began to pour into Nelson’s Senate office. The article piqued the interest of Denis Hayes, then a graduate student at Harvard. Hayes traveled to Washington DC, and arranged a 10-minute visit with Senator Nelson (which stretched into two hours).<sup id="cite_ref-:18_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-21">[21]</a></sup> Hayes returned to Harvard with the charter to organize Boston. After a few days of reference checks,<sup id="cite_ref-:19_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-22">[22]</a></sup> he was asked to drop out of Harvard to become executive director of the national campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Because of the non-hierarchical tenor of the times, Hayes suggested that people be designated coordinators rather than directors. He became the national coordinator,<sup id="cite_ref-:20_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-24">[24]</a></sup> and he quickly hired various regional coordinators, a press coordinator, a K-12 coordinator, a volunteer coordinator, etc. At its peak, the national office had a few dozen paid staff, each earning a flat $375/month, plus more than 100 regular volunteers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>As the talented regional coordinators fanned out across the country, however, they immediately encountered two problems. First, by 1970, the concept of “teach-ins” had become passé. Moreover, teach-ins generally involved debates, and no one was pro-pollution. Second, and more troubling, leading activists on college campuses were deeply involved in the anti-war and civil rights movements. They tended to view the environment as a distraction.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="The_.E2.80.9CEarth_Day.E2.80.9D_name"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_“Earth_Day”_name">The “Earth Day” name</span></h3> 
      <p>The solution to the first problem came from an unexpected direction. Shortly after the turn of the year, a quiet man named Julien Koenig stopped by the national offices and volunteered to help. Koenig was a Madison Avenue giant. His campaign for Volkswagen, “Think Small,” was later cited by Advertising Age as the “greatest advertising campaign of the 20th century.”<sup id="cite_ref-:21_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Over coffee, Hayes confided that the “teach-in” moniker was not working and asked whether Koenig had any ideas. Koenig asked for a few days. A week later, he returned with an assortment of mock-ups for ads, laid out around the announcement of “Ecology Day,” “Environment Day,” “Earth Day,” and “E Day.” He said his personal favorite was Earth Day. Hayes immediately agreed. Koenig offered to prepare a fully refined ad. Hayes insisted that it include a small coupon soliciting funds for the threadbare operation. Koenig ad was visually arresting, and perfectly summed up the issues and values, the feisty-but-welcoming tone that the campaign had adopted. Hayes loved it and decided to bet the farm. He committed about half of all the money in the campaign’s bank account to buy a full-page in the <i>Sunday New York Times</i> opinion section.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The ad was a huge success. Overnight, “Earth Day” became the almost-universally-used name for the upcoming event. The ad generated more than enough revenue to repay its cost, and thousands of potential organizers sent in their names and addresses along with their checks. In future months, magazines and alternative newspapers ran the ad for free, generating still more names and more financial support. The national office started using Environmental Action, rather than Environmental Teach-in, on its letterhead and publications to promote Earth Day.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-26">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At this point, Hayes made a far-reaching decision. In those early days, it would have been easy to obtain trademark protection for Earth Day and force compliance with a set of standards by anyone using it. Hayes decided, however, that he wanted the name to be broadly used by anyone who planned to focus on environmental issues that spring.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Although “Earth Day” swiftly replaced Environmental Teach-in, the second problem proved more complicated. College activists, for the most part, viewed anything other than ending the war as a distraction. A majority of the Earth Day staff had cut their teeth as organizers against the war and saw no conflict. The war appeared to be winding down, and they felt it was prudent to start paying attention to the far more profound changes needed to produce a healthy, sustainable America. But time was short, and college activists were not responding.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Hayes spent a day reviewing the letters Senator Nelson had received, and discovered that very few were from college students. Most were from women who appeared to be college-educated homemakers who wanted to do something to improve the world for their children. Another large share was from K-12 teachers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Hayes decided to shift the campaign’s focus from colleges and universities to community organizing. Building off the successful strategies of the anti-war movement and the civil rights movement, he decided to promote large urban rallies, focused on major environmental issues, while also encouraging environmental education at the K-12 level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Bryce Hamilton, who had been Midwest coordinator, was shifted to K-12 coordinator, and it proved to be a great choice.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-28">[28]</a></sup> Hamilton reached out to the National Education Association, American Federation of Teachers, and the National Science Teachers Association to enlist their members; he provided materials to thousands of educators who wrote to the group directly; and he distributed the most creative ideas he received from anyone to everyone else. In April, more than 10,000 primary and secondary schools engaged in Earth Day activities, mostly education and service actions like beach clean-ups, tree planting, and recycling.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Walt_Kelly" title="Walt Kelly">Walt Kelly</a> created an anti-pollution poster featuring his comic strip character Pogo with the quotation "<a href="/wiki/We_have_met_the_enemy_and_he_is_us" class="mw-redirect" title="We have met the enemy and he is us">We have met the enemy and he is us</a>" to promote the 1970 Earth Day. Environmental groups have sought to make Earth Day into a day of action to change human behavior and provoke policy changes.<sup id="cite_ref-:24aa_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24aa-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first Earth Day "brought 20 million Americans out into the spring sunshine for peaceful demonstrations in favor of environmental reform."<sup id="cite_ref-:24bb_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24bb-30">[30]</a></sup> Earth Day is now observed in 192 countries, and coordinated by the nonprofit EARTHDAY.ORG (formerly Earth Day Network). According to Denis Hayes, the first Earth Day 1970 organizer and current Board Chair Emeritus of EARTHDAY.ORG, Earth Day is now "the largest secular day of protest in the world, and more than a billion people participate in Earth Day actions every year."<sup id="cite_ref-:24CB_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24CB-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By far the largest source of funding for the first Earth Day was organized labor. The United Auto Workers (UAW) had been led by Walter Reuther since 1946, and he was a progressive supporter of civil rights, opposed the war, and championed the environment. He was a founding member of the Coalition for Clean Air, which successfully lobbied for the Clean Air Act of 1970. Walter Reuther, president of the United Auto Workers, made the first donation to support the first Earth Day in the amount of $2,000.<sup id="cite_ref-:24MM_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24MM-32">[32]</a></sup> Under his leadership, the UAW also funded telephone capabilities so that the organizers could communicate and coordinate with each other from all across the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-:24MLM_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24MLM-33">[33]</a></sup> The UAW also financed, printed, and mailed all of the literature and other materials for the first Earth Day and mobilized its members to participate in the public demonstrations across the country. According to Denis Hayes, "The UAW was by far the largest contributor to the first Earth Day" and "Without the UAW, the first Earth Day would have likely flopped!" Hayes further said, "Walter’s presence at our first press conference utterly changed the dynamics of the coverage—we had instant credibility."<sup id="cite_ref-:24MZ_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24MZ-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At a meeting of the Environmental Teach-In board of directors, the chair of the finance committee arrived with a check for $20,000 from Standard Oil of New Jersey (now ExxonMobil). That would have provided a powerful measure of financial relief for the financially strapped group. But Hayes declined the check, convincing the board that it would destroy the credibility of the nascent organization. He said that he would be delighted to accept money from clean sources, but no other corporate money was ever raised for the national organization.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Individual donations were a significant source of funding, generally accompanied with a contribution slip from the Earth Day Ad providing the donor’s name and address. Larry Rockefeller persuaded Robert Rauschenberg to create and donate a batch of Earth Day lithographs, but the Earth Day staff lacked contacts in the art world who were able to sell them for their $2,000 market value, so they were provided to donors for much less.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The sale of standard posters and especially pins brought in additional revenue. The staff refused to sell bumper strips because they would be attached to cars.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Dirty_Dozen">The Dirty Dozen</span></h3> 
      <p>The staff of Environmental Teach-In resigned immediately after Earth Day and most moved directly to a new organization, Environmental Action, with a tax status that permitted lobbying and a more activist stance.<sup id="cite_ref-:1a_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-35">[35]</a></sup> EA immediately confronted a problem that had been looming in the background throughout the campaign. Some of the staff had been drawn to the movement through science and culture and felt that politics was inherently dirty and government was irredeemably compromised. This group believed that by living lives of voluntary simplicity, employing tools like those that filled the resolutely-nonpolitical <i><a href="/wiki/Whole_Earth_Catalog" title="Whole Earth Catalog">Whole Earth Catalog</a></i>, they could force the world to adapt to them. Their theory of change was modeled loosely on the southern African Americans who sat at segregated lunch counters, drank from segregated lunch counters, and sat in the front of the bus, it ignored the role of strategic litigation<sup id="cite_ref-:3a_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3a-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">federal legislation</a>, and electoral politics<sup id="cite_ref-:5a_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5a-37">[37]</a></sup> in cementing lasting change. </p>
      <p>Other staff members had worked in the Robert Kennedy, Gene McCarthy, and various congressional campaigns before Earth Day. They believed that lasting progress could only come through institutional change. 1970 was a Congressional election year. They had just organized the largest demonstration in the nation’s history in order to support environmental values. Former Lindsay organizer, Steve Haft, summed up this faction’s attitude at an Environmental Action staff meeting, “We had 20 million people in the streets in an election year, and you plan to sit out the election? Are you nuts?”<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In order to square the circle, Hayes proposed that the group not endorse any candidates but that it try to defeat 12 of the worst. If having a terrible environmental record became a political liability, it would inevitably lead to better environmental legislation. Haft was selected to coordinate the Dirty Dozen campaign. With just $50,000 to defeat 12 incumbent members of the House, the odds were long. <sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>To improve the odds, the group selected candidates who not only had lousy environmental records—which were plentiful—but who also had won their most recent race by a narrow margin; who were on the wrong side of an important environmental issue in their districts; and who lived in areas where talented Earth Day organizers resided. In the end, seven of the original Dirty Dozen were defeated—five Republicans and two Democrats. And the first to fall was George Fallon, chairman of the hugely powerful House Public Works Committee.<sup id="cite_ref-:6a_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6a-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Representative Pete McCloskey, Earth Day co-chair, credits the Dirty Dozen’s defeat of key Congressional leaders with the unstoppable wave of environmental legislation that immediately followed: the Clean Air Act, Clean Water Act, Endangered Species Act, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-:7a_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7a-40">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_University_of_Michigan_Teach-in">The University of Michigan Teach-in</span></h3> 
      <p>As the tone of major planned Earth Day activities shifted to become less academic and more confrontational, and the Environmental Action newsletter emphasized the need for broad structural change, interest in the event began to mount among college student activists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>One place where the interest in a teach-in was robust from the beginning was the University of Michigan. The very first teach-in on the Vietnam War had been held at the University of Michigan in March 1965, and a group of students, led by Doug Scott,<sup id="cite_ref-:26_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:26-41">[41]</a></sup> decided to mark the 5-year anniversary with an environmental teach-in on March 11–14, 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-:27_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:27-42">[42]</a></sup> The Michigan teach-in presented a series of speeches dealing with a variety of environmental problems, along with some debate over the best tactics and solutions. No one, including the president of Dow Chemical, argued for more environmental destruction. </p>
      <p>After the University of Michigan Teach-in, there was an explosion of interest on other college campuses. Upwards of 2,000 universities, colleges, and junior colleges ultimately put on events. By the end, the national staff had a hard time merely keeping up with the colleges that called to register events.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Focus_on_Pollution">The Focus on Pollution</span></h3> 
      <p>The delicate line straddled by organizers was to attract the seasoned activists who would demand far-reaching change without alienating the middle class whose active participation and political support were seen as essential. The greatest environmental insults were visited on the poor. Factories and power plants were located in the poorest neighborhoods. Freeways were plowed through the poorest neighborhoods. Toxic waste dumps were situated in the poorest neighborhoods. But these problems tended not to affect the middle class.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The solution was to promote an overarching concern with air and water pollution, which affected everyone, while encouraging each community to pay attention to whatever other issues were of most concern to it. Earth Day included events that focused on fighting freeways, protecting the ozone layer, organic food, whales and endangered species, oil spills, the military use of Agent Orange in Vietnam, overpopulation, peeling lead paint in ghetto housing, opposition to the Supersonic Transport, and myriad other topics. At one event, college students would pound an automobile apart with sledge hammers <sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>, or, wearing gas masks, would block traffic on a freeway. At other events, grade-school students would plant trees, pick up litter in city parks, or identify birds. Earth Day welcomed them all.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Regional coordinators focused heavily on finding and enlisting the best local leadership in major metropolitan areas. For instance, Hayes flew to Chicago to help organize a subtle coup, replacing a pro-business Earth Day organization with a <a href="/wiki/Saul_Alinsky" title="Saul Alinsky">Saul Alinsky</a> affiliated group called Campaign Against Pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-:29_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:29-44">[44]</a></sup> CAP abruptly shifted the focus away from recycling to focus on two issues: opposition to a massive proposed freeway program, the <a href="/wiki/Crosstown_Expressway_(Chicago)" title="Crosstown Expressway (Chicago)">Crosstown Expressway</a>, and protesting the uncontrolled air pollution Commonwealth Edison was pouring into Chicago’s air—more sulfur pollution than all other companies combined.<sup id="cite_ref-:31_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:31-45">[45]</a></sup> Although mailings went out to thousands of communities of all sizes, the campaign focused especially hard on large cities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_City">New York City</span></h3> 
      <p>In the winter of 1969–1970, a group of students met at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> to hear Denis Hayes talk about his plans for Earth Day. Among the group were <a href="/wiki/Fred_Kent" title="Fred Kent">Fred Kent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Grannis" title="Pete Grannis">Pete Grannis</a>, and Kristin and William Hubbard. This group agreed to head up the New York City activities within the national movement. Fred Kent took the lead in renting an office and recruiting volunteers. The liberal Republican mayor of New York, John Lindsay, saw the environment as an issue that could help unite his then-troubled city. Moreover, he viewed the environment as a progressive wedge issue that would position him as clearly distinct from President Nixon’s ultra-conservative “Southern Strategy,”<sup id="cite_ref-:32_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:32-46">[46]</a></sup> in a struggle for the soul of the Republican Party. He became fully engaged in supporting the event, and he delegated many of the talented young staff who had been drawn to his administration to help as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg/150px-Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg/225px-Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg/300px-Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg 2x" data-file-width="324" data-file-height="475"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ed-Muskie-at-Earth-Day-1970-web.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Muskie" title="Edmund Muskie">Edmund Muskie</a> speaking at Fairmount Park, Philadelphia on Earth Day, 1970
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>"The big break came when Mayor Lindsay agreed to shut down Fifth Avenue for the event. A giant cheer went up in the office on that day," according to Kristin Hubbard (now <a href="/wiki/Kristin_Kuhns_Alexandre" title="Kristin Kuhns Alexandre">Kristin Alexandre</a>). "From that time on we used Mayor Lindsay's offices and even his staff. I was Speaker Coordinator but had tremendous help from Lindsay staffer Judith Crichton." Mayor Lindsay completely closed down Fifth Avenue to traffic from E. 14th Street to West 59th Street (Central Park)—more than 2 miles—and 14th Street between 3rd and 7th Avenues.<sup id="cite_ref-:33_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:33-47">[47]</a></sup> An estimated one million participants took part—right in the nerve center of the nation’s communications complex.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In addition to shutting down <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a>, Mayor <a href="/wiki/John_Lindsay" title="John Lindsay">John Lindsay</a> made <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a> available for Earth Day. In <a href="/wiki/Union_Square,_Manhattan" title="Union Square, Manhattan">Union Square</a>, the <i>New York Times</i> estimated crowds of up to 20,000 people at any given time and, perhaps, more than 100,000 over the course of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-:33AB_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:33AB-48">[48]</a></sup> Since <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> was also the home of <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i>, it provided the best possible anchor for national coverage from their reporters throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:33CC_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:33CC-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</span></h3> 
      <p>U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Muskie" title="Edmund Muskie">Edmund Muskie</a> was the keynote speaker on Earth Day in <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a> in Philadelphia. Other notable attendees included consumer protection activist and presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, landscape architect <a href="/wiki/Ian_McHarg" title="Ian McHarg">Ian McHarg</a>, Nobel prize-winning Harvard biochemist <a href="/wiki/George_Wald" title="George Wald">George Wald</a>, U.S. Senate Minority Leader <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Scott" title="Hugh Scott">Hugh Scott</a>, and poet <a href="/wiki/Allen_Ginsberg" title="Allen Ginsberg">Allen Ginsberg</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_1980">Earth Day 1980</span></h2> 
      <p>The 1970s were a period of substantial environmental legislation, including the Clean Air Act, Clean Water Act, Endangered Species Act, Marine Mammal Protection Act, Superfund, Toxics Substances Control Act, and the Resource Conservation and Recovery Act. It had seen the creation of the Environmental Protection Agency and the banning of DDT and of lead in gasoline. Jimmy Carter was President. </p>
      <p>The 1980 Earth Day effort was led by <a href="/wiki/W._Michael_McCabe" title="W. Michael McCabe">Mike McCabe</a> and Byron Kennard,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and the general mood was festive and celebratory. The principal Washington, DC event was a festival<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> held in Lafayette Park, across the street from the White House. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_1990_to_1999">Earth Day 1990 to 1999</span></h2> 
      <p>Mobilizing 200 million people in 141 countries and lifting the status of <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_issues" title="List of environmental issues">environmental issues</a> onto the world stage, Earth Day activities in 1990 gave a huge boost to recycling efforts worldwide and helped pave the way for the 1992 United Nations <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> in Rio de Janeiro. Unlike the first Earth Day in 1970, this 20th Anniversary was waged with stronger marketing tools, greater access to television and radio, and multimillion-dollar budgets.<sup id="cite_ref-NEW_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEW-52">[52]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg/150px-Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg/225px-Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg/300px-Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="669"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ed20-peace-climb-logo-web.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The official logo of the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> <a href="/wiki/Earth_Day_20_International_Peace_Climb" title="Earth Day 20 International Peace Climb">Earth Day 20 International Peace Climb</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Two separate groups formed to sponsor Earth Day events in 1990: The Earth Day 20 Foundation, assembled by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Furia" title="Edward Furia">Edward Furia</a> (Project Director of Earth Week in 1970), and Earth Day 1990, assembled by <a href="/wiki/Denis_Hayes" title="Denis Hayes">Denis Hayes</a> (National Coordinator for Earth Day 1970). Senator <a href="/wiki/Gaylord_Nelson" title="Gaylord Nelson">Gaylord Nelson</a>, the original founder of Earth Day, was honorary chairman for both groups. The two did not combine forces over disagreements about leadership of combined organization and incompatible structures and strategies. Among the disagreements, key Earth Day 20 Foundation organizers were critical of Earth Day 1990 for including on their board <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a>, a company that at the time was the second-biggest emitter of chlorofluorocarbons in Silicon Valley and refused to switch to alternative solvents. In terms of marketing, Earth Day 20 had a grassroots approach to organizing and relied largely on locally based groups like the National Toxics Campaign, a Boston-based coalition of 1,000 local groups concerned with industrial pollution. Earth Day 1990 employed strategies including focus group testing, direct mail fund raising, and email marketing.<sup id="cite_ref-NEW_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEW-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Earth Day 20 Foundation highlighted its April 22 activities in <a href="/wiki/George,_Washington" title="George, Washington">George, Washington</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a> with a live satellite phone call with members of the historic <a href="/wiki/Earth_Day_20_International_Peace_Climb" title="Earth Day 20 International Peace Climb">Earth Day 20 International Peace Climb</a> who called from their base camp on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> to pledge their support for world peace and attention to environmental issues.<sup id="cite_ref-GG_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG-53">[53]</a></sup> The Earth Day 20 International Peace Climb was led by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Whittaker" title="Jim Whittaker">Jim Whittaker</a>, the first American to summit Mt. Everest (many years earlier), and marked the first time in history that mountaineers from the United States, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and China had roped together to climb a mountain, let alone Mt. Everest.<sup id="cite_ref-GG_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG-53">[53]</a></sup> The group also collected more than two tons of trash (transported down the mountain by support groups along the way) that was left behind on Mount Everest from previous climbing expeditions. The master of ceremonies for the Columbia Gorge event was the TV star, <a href="/wiki/John_Ratzenberger" title="John Ratzenberger">John Ratzenberger</a>, from <i><a href="/wiki/Cheers" title="Cheers">Cheers</a></i>, and the headlining musician was the "Father of Rock and Roll", <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GG_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Bros. Records">Warner Bros. Records</a> released an Earth Day-themed single in 1990 entitled "Tomorrow's World", written by <a href="/wiki/Kix_Brooks" title="Kix Brooks">Kix Brooks</a> (who would later become one-half of <a href="/wiki/Brooks_%26_Dunn" title="Brooks &amp; Dunn">Brooks &amp; Dunn</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Pam_Tillis" title="Pam Tillis">Pam Tillis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HURST_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HURST-54">[54]</a></sup> The song featured vocals from <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Anderson" title="Lynn Anderson">Lynn Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butch_Baker" title="Butch Baker">Butch Baker</a>, Shane Barmby, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Hill_(band)" title="Billy Hill (band)">Billy Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzy_Bogguss" title="Suzy Bogguss">Suzy Bogguss</a>, Kix Brooks, <a href="/wiki/T._Graham_Brown" title="T. Graham Brown">T. Graham Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Burch_Sisters" title="The Burch Sisters">The Burch Sisters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holly_Dunn" title="Holly Dunn">Holly Dunn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foster_%26_Lloyd" title="Foster &amp; Lloyd">Foster &amp; Lloyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vince_Gill" title="Vince Gill">Vince Gill</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lee_Golden" title="William Lee Golden">William Lee Golden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Highway_101" title="Highway 101">Highway 101</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shelby_Lynne" title="Shelby Lynne">Shelby Lynne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Rodriguez" title="Johnny Rodriguez">Johnny Rodriguez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Seals" title="Dan Seals">Dan Seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Les_Taylor_(singer)" title="Les Taylor (singer)">Les Taylor</a>, Pam Tillis, <a href="/wiki/Mac_Wiseman" title="Mac Wiseman">Mac Wiseman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Welch" title="Kevin Welch">Kevin Welch</a>. It charted at number 74 on the <a href="/wiki/Hot_Country_Songs" title="Hot Country Songs">Hot Country Songs</a> chart dated May 5, 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-WHIT_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHIT-55">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Earth_Day_2000.E2.80.932019"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2000–2019">Earth Day 2000–2019</span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2000">Earth Day 2000</span></h3> 
      <p>As the millennium approached, Hayes agreed to spearhead another campaign, this time focusing on <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a> and pushing for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">clean energy</a>. The April 22 Earth Day in 2000 combined the big-picture feistiness of the first Earth Day with the international grassroots activism of Earth Day 1990. For 2000, Earth Day had the internet to help link activists around the world. By the time April 22 came around, 5,000 environmental groups around the world were on board reaching out to hundreds of millions of people in a record 184 countries. Events varied: A <a href="/wiki/Talking_drum" title="Talking drum">talking drum</a> chain traveled from village to village in <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>, Africa, for example, while hundreds of thousands of people gathered on the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> in Washington, D.C., USA.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2001">Earth Day 2001</span></h3> 
      <p>Google’s first Earth Day doodle was in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-:34_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:34-56">[56]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2003">Earth Day 2003</span></h3> 
      <p>The theme for Earth Day 2003 was the Water for Life Campaign. This year, Earth Day Network developed a water quality project called “What’s in Your Water?”<sup id="cite_ref-:35_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:35-57">[57]</a></sup> Water-related events were held on every continent, including water workshops, exhibitions, concerts, and more in <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:36_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:36-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:37_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:37-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:38_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:38-60">[60]</a></sup> Educational curricula, teacher’s guides, water testing kits, and posters focused on water. </p>
      <p>Campaign for Communities, an initiative led by NAACP, Latino organizations including Southwest Voter Registration and Education Project, and other organizations focused on environmental justice, created events focused on low income communities around the U.S. These events were also focused on building support among low income communities through clean ups, park revitalization, and town halls focused on integrating the environmental movement with community and social justice causes.<sup id="cite_ref-:39_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:39-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:40_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:40-62">[62]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2004">Earth Day 2004</span></h3> 
      <p>In the U.S. in 2004, Earth Day Network and its partners focused on voter registration for Earth Day, registering hundreds of thousands of voters.<sup id="cite_ref-:41_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:41-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:42_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:42-64">[64]</a></sup> Major tree planting events also took place. Other prominent U.S. Earth Day events included an annual cleanup in Dayton, Ohio and the <i>3rd Annual Community Based Solutions to Environmental Health &amp; Justice Conference</i> in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:43_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:43-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:44_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:44-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2005">Earth Day 2005</span></h3> 
      <p>The theme for Earth Day 2005 was Healthy Environments for Children.<sup id="cite_ref-:45_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:45-67">[67]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2006">Earth Day 2006</span></h3> 
      <p>Earth Day 2006 focused on science and faith. Earth Day expanded into Europe for Earth Day 2006 and events and speeches were held in most of the EU countries. Key events included the “Festival on Climate Change” in Utrecht, the Netherlands, which was focused on “How to break away from the oil dependence,” and included Earth Day founder Denis Hayes and members of the Dutch and E.U. parliament, NGOs, local authorities, and media representatives. In the first of two years of Earth Day events in Ukraine, Denis Hayes also attended and spoke at the “Chernobyl 20 Remembrance for the Future” conference in Kiev, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:46_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:46-68">[68]</a></sup> 2006 also saw events in China organized between Earth Day Network and Global Village Beijing educating communities about energy savings, the first ever coordinated Earth Day events in Moscow, Russia, a scientific panel and a religious response panel on climate change throughout the U.S., and a “Conserve Your Energy” event in Philadelphia.<sup id="cite_ref-:47_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:47-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:48_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:48-70">[70]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2007">Earth Day 2007</span></h3> 
      <p>Thousands of Earth Day projects were held across the globe that ranged from energy efficiency events, protests, letter writing campaigns, civic and environmental k-12 education trainings, urban and rural cleanups, and water projects with a particular focus on building a broader and more diverse environmental movement.<sup id="cite_ref-:49_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:49-71">[71]</a></sup> Major events took place in <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a>, Ukraine; <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, Venezuela; <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a>; Philippines; Togo; and <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the US, civil rights, religious, and social justice leaders joined Earth Day Network the week of April 16 through April 20 to demand Congress on behalf of their communities and their constituencies that there be no “grandfathering” of pollution permits, that an immediate reduction in carbon emissions be imposed through legislation and that all revenues generated from a carbon tax or a government auction of carbon permits be used for public benefit. Earth Day Network partnered with <a href="/wiki/Green_Apple_Music_%26_Arts_Festival" title="Green Apple Music &amp; Arts Festival">Green Apple Music &amp; Arts Festival</a> to mark Earth Day with weekend-long events featuring music and entertainment in New York, San Francisco, and Chicago.<sup id="cite_ref-:50_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:50-72">[72]</a></sup> More than 200,000 people attended the events and millions of people were reached through the media. </p>
      <p>The <i>Global Warming in the Pulpit Pledge campaign</i> which mobilized priests, ministers, rabbis, and other faith leaders across the U.S. and Canada to make a commitment to preach on global climate change as a moral issue was launched.<sup id="cite_ref-:51_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:51-73">[73]</a></sup> Later in 2007, <a href="/wiki/Live_Earth_(2007_concert)" title="Live Earth (2007 concert)">Live Earth</a>, a global music event, was held across the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2008">Earth Day 2008</span></h3> 
      <p>Earth Day 2008 galvanized millions of people around the world in a Call For Climate. In the U.S., the campaign challenged the public to make one million calls to Congress about pushing for climate change legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-:52_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:52-74">[74]</a></sup> 2008 also included large climate rallies in eight major U.S. cities including Washington, D.C., New York, Miami, Chicago, Dallas, Denver, Los Angeles, and San Francisco, to which around 350,000 people attended. Washington, D.C. hosted actor Edward Norton, Thomas Friedman of the New York Times, and James Hansen of NASA to deliver a strong global warming message and call for tough and fair climate action by Congress. O.A.R., Umphrey’s McGee, Warren Haynes, Mambo Sauce, and Blake Lewis of American Idol entertained enthusiastic crowds, and the event was covered live by CNN and The Weather Channel plus scores of other media that carried the Call for Climate message.<sup id="cite_ref-:53_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:53-75">[75]</a></sup> Many Earth Day events were held around the world from the Earth Day on Campus campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-:54_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:54-76">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2009">Earth Day 2009</span></h3> 
      <p>The 2009 National Teach-In on Global Warming Solutions reached college campuses, civic organizations, and faith groups across the U.S. As part of this event, members of Congress addressed college and high school campuses in their districts via video conference.<sup id="cite_ref-:55_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:55-77">[77]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Earth_Day_2010_.2840th_anniversary.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2010_(40th_anniversary)">Earth Day 2010 (40th anniversary)</span></h3> 
      <p>An estimated one billion people around the world took action for the 40th anniversary of Earth Day.<sup id="cite_ref-:56_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:56-78">[78]</a></sup> An estimated 20,000 partners took action on climate change and other environmental issues through climate rallies, Billion Acts of Green™, and by engaging civil leaders in plans to build a green economy, connected through the online action center at EARTHDAY.ORG.<sup id="cite_ref-:57_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:57-79">[79]</a></sup> Through the Global Day of Conversation, more than 200 elected officials in more than 39 countries took part in active dialogues with their constituents about their efforts to create sustainable green economies and reduce their carbon footprints.<sup id="cite_ref-:58_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:58-80">[80]</a></sup> Students around the world participated in school greenings, featuring community clean ups, solar energy systems, school gardens, and environmental curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-:59_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:59-81">[81]</a></sup> Earth Day Network announced a partnership with Twentieth Century Fox Home Entertainment’s Avatar Home Tree Initiative to plant one million trees in 15 countries by the end of 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-:60_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:60-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Climate Rally on the National Mall in Washington, D.C. drew in more than 150,000 activists to demand that U.S. Congress pass comprehensive climate legislation in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-:61_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:61-83">[83]</a></sup> The nine-hour event featured more than 70 high profile speakers, including leadership from the faith, labor, civil rights, environmental communities, the private sector, leading climate scientists, celebrities, Cabinet Secretaries, international political leaders, and local government officials.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In partnership with the Peace Corps, Earth Day Network worked with local volunteers to implement environmental and civic education programs, tree plantings, village clean ups, and recycling seminars in rural areas in Ukraine, the Philippines, Georgia, Albania, and Kolkata, India.<sup id="cite_ref-:62_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:62-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2010, Earth Day Network also established a satellite office in Kolkata, India.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>As part of a nationwide commemoration of the 40th anniversary in Morocco, the government announced a unique National Charter for the Environment and Sustainable Development, the first commitment of its kind in Africa and the Arab world, which will inform new environmental laws for the country. The Kingdom of Morocco also pledged to plant one million trees.<sup id="cite_ref-:63_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:63-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2011">Earth Day 2011</span></h3> 
      <p>2011 Earth Day events included an environmental forum for local political leaders and the first ever Earth Day celebration in Tunis City and primary school events throughout Iraq. In 17 of the world’s most severely deforested countries, Earth Day Network completed a project to plant over 1.1 million trees. Across the globe, more than 100 million Billion Acts of Green were registered.<sup id="cite_ref-:64_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:64-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:65_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:65-87">[87]</a></sup> In September 2011, at the Clinton Global Initiative, President Clinton recognized this project as an exemplary approach to addressing global challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-:66_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:66-88">[88]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2012">Earth Day 2012</span></h3> 
      <p>A Billion Acts of Green® were achieved on Earth Day 2012, with Earth Day Network announcing the accomplishment at the United Nations Conference on Sustainable Development in Rio.<sup id="cite_ref-:67_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:67-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:68_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:68-90">[90]</a></sup> A Billion Acts of Green® is billed as the world’s largest environmental service campaign, inspiring and rewarding both simple individual acts and larger organizational initiatives that further the goal of measurably reducing carbon emissions and supporting sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-:69_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:69-91">[91]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-:70_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:70-92">[92]</a></sup> The Campaign for Communities engaged elected officials in finding solutions to local environmental challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-:71_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:71-93">[93]</a></sup> Faith programs saw Catholic parishes and churches across the U.S. take action on Earth Day through sermons and other activities, including four events at the National Cathedral and a conference at the St. Sophia Cathedral and National Religious Coalition on Creation Care (NRCC).<sup id="cite_ref-:72_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:72-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:73_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:73-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:74_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:74-96">[96]</a></sup> Mobilize the Earth™ events throughout all of India’s states and geographic regions, coordinated by Earth Day Network India, included rallies, concerts, competitions, seminars, art shows, plantation drives, signature campaigns, and workshops.<sup id="cite_ref-:75_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:75-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:76_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:76-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:77_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:77-99">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2013">Earth Day 2013</span></h3> 
      <p>The goal of Earth Day 2013 was to personalize the massive challenge that climate change presents, while uniting people around the globe into a powerful call to action with the theme: The Face of Climate Change. To illustrate that climate change is not a remote problem for our leaders but is impacting real people, animals, and places everywhere, EDN collected images sent into #faceofclimate and displayed them in a collage at thousands of events around the world—from schools to parks to government buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-:78_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:78-100">[100]</a></sup> High level organizations and individuals participated in the campaign including the Secretary General of the Organization of American States, US Secretary of State, and many more.<sup id="cite_ref-:79_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:79-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Meanwhile, stories of hope inspired communities to take action<sup id="cite_ref-:80_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:80-102">[102]</a></sup> and MobilizeU, a program to educate and activate college students around Earth Day and environmental issues, expanded in 2013 to 296 universities in 51 countries on six continents and in 46 US states.<sup id="cite_ref-:81_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:81-103">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Washington, D.C., EDN presented Earth Month at Union Station, a four-week series of events that featured an environmental film festival, renewable energy demonstrations, farmers markets, NASA educational exhibits, and public talks by scientists and astronauts. In partnership with Washington, D.C. Public Schools, EDN also presented a STEM Fair at Union Station.<sup id="cite_ref-:82_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:82-104">[104]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2014">Earth Day 2014</span></h3> 
      <p>The goal of Earth Day 2014 was to dramatically personalize the massive challenges surrounding global climate change and weave that into both Earth Day 2014 and the five-year countdown to Earth Day 2020, the 50th anniversary. It was an opportunity to unite people worldwide into a common cause and call for action.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The theme of Earth Day 2014 was Green Cities.<sup id="cite_ref-:83_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:83-105">[105]</a></sup> Earth Day Network launched the Green Cities campaign in the fall of 2013 to help cities around the world become more sustainable and reduce their carbon footprint. Focused on three key elements – buildings, energy, and transportation – the campaign aims to help cities accelerate their transition to a cleaner, healthier, and more economically viable future through improvements in efficiency, investments in renewable technology, and regulation reform.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2015">Earth Day 2015</span></h3> 
      <p>To recognize the 45th anniversary of Earth Day, the 2015 global theme was termed “It’s Our Turn to Lead.”<sup id="cite_ref-:84_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:84-106">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>That year, Earth Day was a part of the steady drumbeat towards Paris for the UNFCCC COP 21 climate talks that December. With a binding treaty on climate change expected from this conference, this is a pivotal year for the environmental movement. Earth Day Network’s (EDN) campaigns focused on instigating environmental advocacy, as well as strengthening climate communication and education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>EDN designed and executed four campaigns for its major constituencies: Green Cities, MobilizeU, Climate Education Week, and Faith Mobilization, all of which concentrated on the organization’s theme: “It’s Our Turn to Lead”.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In addition, EDN hosted Global Citizen 2015 Earth Day,<sup id="cite_ref-:85_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:85-107">[107]</a></sup> in partnership with Global Poverty Project (GPP) on the National Mall in Washington, D.C. This venue allowed EDN and GPP to inspire and activate a new generation of global activists. The event worked to establish the intersection between eradicating global poverty and stopping climate change. Earth Day Network worked directly with major partners to garner large-scale commitments to stop climate change.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2016">Earth Day 2016</span></h3> 
      <p>The theme for Earth Day 2016 was Trees for Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-:86_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:86-108">[108]</a></sup> India, the Caribbean, Vietnam, and Morocco made substantial government commitments. The Black Lives Matter organization, which was created in 2013, also engaged with EarthDay.org in community panels. Close to 700 million community members, organizations and school children took part in Earth Day 2016. Over 36 million trees were planted in India. “Earth Day Network” (now EARTHDAY.ORG) was accepted as an entry on Baidu Baike, a major online cyclopedia in China.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Hundreds of mayors across the world participated in Earth Day 2016 primarily focused on urban planning and reforestation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signing_of_the_Paris_Agreement_at_the_UN">Signing of the Paris Agreement at the UN</span></h3> 
      <p>It was no accident that the United Nations selected Earth Day<sup id="cite_ref-:87_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:87-109">[109]</a></sup> to sign the most significant climate accord in the history of the climate movement. On Earth Day 2016, world leaders from 175 nations broke a record by doing exactly that. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_Day_2017">Earth Day 2017</span></h3> 
      <table class="box-Unreferenced plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
